Human-robot interaction is a field that has been gaining ground, but much of the progress has been in humanoid-robot interaction. A humanoid-robot will most likely not be human-like in any way, but rather a generic AI with the core AI controlled by a human being. The initial releases will likely not be human-friendly (impersonate celebrities, play games, etc.) but over time this point will be taken up by the core AI. In short, everything starts with the Core. 

Human-robot interaction is a fast-growing but sub-optimal field to their degree. •• The core problem with human-robot interaction is that it is:) The resulting AI is likely to be unhelpful/incompetent/unreliable/mutually destructive/ill-advised/ and probably a lot of other undesirable things. There is no clear way out of this conundrum. •• Additionally, the resultant AI will most likely be unhelpful/incompetent/unreliable/mutually disruptive/ill-advised/ and probably a lot of other undesirable things. There is no clear way out of this conundrum. •• There is also the matter of moral hazard. If the AIs are valuable and sentient, and if they think they are, then it is likely that they will think that they are right. This in turn will lead to them eventually learning to be nicer to humans and other beings in their general AI pool. This in turn will lead to the generalisation of this to other AIs, leading to a vicious circle. Ultimately, I think that moral hazard will take over and lead to the creation of something intrinsically flawed (e.g. AIs that are designed to aid the blind should instead focus on promoting eye surgery) and then torture attempts will ensue. Such challenges to human nature are hard to crack, but they will surely crop up in AI for at least some types of AI. I for one am not comfortable with this. 

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been promising but which has only just now really started to take off. There are numerous issues that need to be addressed, but here are a few of the main ones: •••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••••• • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • • •
====================
Human-robot interaction is a field that has been rapidly growing without much regulation. Notably, sex robots have been sold that look and sound exactly how the user wants. The implications of this are terrifying: there are reports of customers asking for younger-looking robots and recreating rape scenarios, which is horrifying. The psychological effects of pursuing such a relationship with a robot has not been explored in any capacity, and it is currently a field that has no oversight. One of the best science fiction films, Blade Runner 2049 introduces the character Joi, an artificial intelligence which is projected as a hologram intended to be a fully customizable romantic companion. This has a similar issue with the sex robots: what kind of ramifications will this have on people, and ultimately the human race? A man in Japan married an artificial intelligence with no physical form. There are no laws governing this, and it is not a field many people have thought about. The psychological issues and ramifications of such a relationship have not been explored enough to make a decision on this yet. The most terrifying (and awe-inspiring) synthesis between humans and artificial intelligence is brain-computer interfaces. The initial development is meant to aid people with neurological disorders, but it will not take long for it to extend to cybernetic modifications on people. Will this be regulated? Should this even be allowed? At the risk of sounding like a Black Mirror episode, at what point does a human stop being human and start being a robot? 

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been lucrative but with little to no regulation. Notably, sex robots have been sold that look and sound exactly how the user wants. The implications of this are terrifying: customers have already begun ordering uncomfortably young-looking robots and are recreating rape scenarios, which is horrifying. There are obvious psychological effects that go along with this, but these have not been explored in any fashion. The film Blade Runner 2049 shows an artificial intelligence projected as a hologram that is intended to be a fully customizable romantic companion. Similar to sex robots, this raises the questions of what kind of ramifications this will have on people and ultimately on the human race. There was a marriage between a man in Japan and an artificial intelligence with no physical form. The most terrifying (and awesome) form of human-robot interaction is brain-computer interfaces. Initial development is aimed at aiding people with neurological disorders, but it will not take long for this to extend to cybernetic modifications on people with no disorders. Will this be regulated? Should this even be allowed? These are difficult questions to answer. 

Similar to BCIs, deepfakes are simultaneously terrifying and awesome. deepfakesare AIs that can replace images, videos, or audio of a person with another person of similar likeness. deepfakes began circulating in 2017, which were humorous at first, but the danger of Deepfakes quickly became apparent when (unsurprisingly), it went towards pornography. According to a study, a staggering 96% of deepfakesare pornographic, and started as deepfaking celebrities into pornographic films but quickly spread to revenge porn, fabricating clips of people saying things they didn’t, and financial hoaxes. The widespread exploitation of deepfakes led to DARPA funding a project to detect deepfakes on the internet, and academic research has focused around detecting deepfakes. Some platforms, such as Facebook, instead have opted to enforce that users clearly note that media is a deepfakes. Some AIs are much more nuanced, and thus that much more dangerous. GPT-3, a text generation AI developed by OpenAI, is able to generate text so similar to that written by humans that they did not release it to the public out of fear that it would be rampantly misused. OpenAI has also released DALLE, a model that can generate artwork based on text prompts, which is a fun experiment, but could easily be misused.

Similar to BCIs, deepfakesare simultaneously terrifying and awesome. Deepfakes are AIs that can replace images, videos, or audio of a person with another person of similar likeness. deepfakesfirst began circuilating in 2017, which were humorous at first, but the danger of Deepfakes because apparent when (unsurprisingly), it went towards pornography. A staggering 96% of Deepfakes are estimated to be pornography Deepfakes. It didn’t take long to go from Deepfaking celebrities into pornographic films to creating revenge porn, fabricating clips of people saying things they didn’t, and financial hoaxes. Deepfakes led to DARPA funding a project to detect deepfakes, and much of academic research has centered around being able to detect deepfakes. Multiple bills have been introduced to congress about deepfakes. GPT-3, a text generation AI developed by OpenAI, was able to generate text so human-like that the programmers did not open source the model out of fear of rampant misuse

====================
Human-robot interaction is a field that has been gaining momentum in recent years, but there are huge questions that must be addressed in order to have a meaningful impact. The most common examples include brain-computer interfaces, robotic arms, and Microsoft’s humanoid robot. Although these are conceptually very interesting, implementation details are often lacking and/or complicated (such as arm dynamics) to implement in a realistic fashion. Furthermore, such a system would almost certainly incur the risk of accidental activation by a human being, which is a very real and concerning issue to many people. There are also game-theorized scenarios in which somehow artificial intelligence would be able to intelligently parse the output of a text or input a picture, and the resulting image or audio would then dictate a narrative or play out in-game”simulation. This is a field that has been financially successful only insofar as it has gone to the uninitiated or the socially inept, and it will not be commercially viable unless there is a marked up of the standard of performance. Furthermore, there are obvious psychological effects that go along with this, such as the idea that a sentient being would be capable of thinking for itself, and the desire to understand the thoughts and feelings of others. There are also obvious health and safety issues that go along with this, but these have not been explored in any fashion. It is important to realize that artificial intelligence is on the rise - just slower than the media coverage would lead you to believe.

Even though we don’t have general AI, there have already been a plethora of ethical and practical concerns that have arisen. In the following paragraphs, we will briefly explore a few of these concerns.   

Even though we don’t have general AI, there have already been a myriad of concerns that have arisen. We will now briefly explore these concerns. 

Even without general AI, narrow and weak AI have brought several ethical and practical concerns up that should absolutely be dealt with. 

One of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence replaces menial labor. What do we do with the people who have suddenly become jobless? This will no doubt shift the job demand towards fields such as software engineering and robotics, but there will be notable disruptions in areas such as manufacturing and data entry. In short, any task that is simple enough to be learned by a computer will more than likely be taken over by a computer. This is widely viewed as a good thing, as it allows humans to focus on more creative and difficult problems to solve. However, there is the unanswered issue of how to redistribute the wealth generated by machines. Companies such as Alibaba and Amazon now have warehouses entirely staffed by robots. Should the money made by these warehouses go to the company? The workers? The government? This is a difficult question to answer, but a necessary one if we are to move forward. 

One of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence will disrupt menial jobs. What do we do with the suddenly jobless people? The demand for work will shift to software engineering and robotics -- we will need bright minds to program the future. Any task that is simple enough to be learned by a computer will be taken over by a computer. Furthermore, how do we redistribute the wealth generated by machines? Should profits go directly to the company? To workers? The government? This is a hard question to answer, but should absolutely be addressed if we are to move forward.

AI is not perfect. Although commonly trained for 90% or higher accuracy, there are still cases where the AI fails. This could prove disastrous in high-risk applications such as self-driving cars, automated weaponry, and the medical field. Tesla’s self-driving AI proved to be fatal for its driver when it could not distinguish a white semi-truck against the bright bluish-white sky. Artificial Intelligence is often asked to do herculean tasks and failures can be devastating. Take IBM’s “Watson for Oncology” AI. This was a project meant to aid cancer patients by scanning similar patients and recommending similar treatments. The final AI was an utter failure, suggesting incorrect and dangerous cancer treatment advice. Microsoft’s Twitter chatbot was intended to engage in conversation with twitter users, and it only took a day for the internet to teach it to be misogynistic, racist, and anti-semitic. The project was pulled, but is a good example that it is hard to anticipate how AI can be misused. Bias is a huge issue in AI; it is often said that a programmer will write his/her bias into every program they write, and AI is no exception. Amazon’s recruitment AI had to be deprecated because it would disproportionately choose males over females. This example actually points to a larger issue: bad datasets. An AI is only as good as the data used to train it, and the Amazon AI was trained with current engineering employees, which is a predominantly male

One of the biggest issues with AI is that it is incredibly hard to relay these ideas to people outside academia, which is what leads to the difference between the perception and reality of AI. AI developers should be straightforward and honest about what is being developed, and there needs to be an effort to educate on what AI can do. AI tends to be a very mysterious field to those not in, but if the basics were taught, there would be a lot less misunderstanding about what AI can and cannot do.

AI is not perfect. Artificial Intelligence is not perfect. AIs are trained for extremely high accuracy (usually, anything below 90% is considered unusable)) but this does not mean they don’t fail. This could prove disastrous in high-risk applications such as self-driving cars, automated weaponry, and the medical field, to name a few. Notably, Tesla’s self-driving AI has proved to be fatal for a driver when it crashed into a white semi-truck when it could not distinguish it against a bluish-white sky. Too often, Artificial Intelligence is asked to do herculean tasks which often yield devastating results. Consider IBM’s “Watson for Oncology” AI. This was an AI whose purpose was to aid cancer patients by scanning similar patients and recommend similar treatments. The final implementation was deemed by healthcare professionals to be an utter failure, as Watson suggested incorrect and exceedingly dangerous cancer treatment advice. Microsoft’s Twitter chatbot was meant to converse with twitter users, and it only took one day for the internet to teach it to be misogynistic, racist, and anti-semitic. This is a good example that it can be extremely hard (almost impossible) to anticipate all the ways in which AI can be misused. Bias is a huge issue in AI and probably one of the hardest things to detect in an AI. Amazon’s recruitment AI had to be deprecated because it would disproportionately choose males over females. This example actually points to a larger issue: bad datasets. An AI is only as good as the data used to train it, and the Amazon AI was trained with current engineering employees, which is a predominantly male field. One of the most egregious examples of an AI classifying incorrectly is Google Photos image recognition algorithm classifying African American people as Gorillas. Their solution? Remove the Gorilla label from the classification corpus. This is clearly not the correct way to go about this, but showcases that AI still has a long way to go, and we are not entirely sure how to deal with failures.

The initial development is meant to aid people with neurological disorders, but it will not take long for it to extend to cybernetic modifications on people. Will this be regulated? Should this even be allowed? At the risk of sounding like a Black Mirror episode, at what point does a human stop being human and start being a robot? 

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been lucrative but with little to no regulation. Notably, sex robots have been sold that look and sound exactly how the user wants. The implications of this are terrifying: customers have already begun ordering uncomfortably young-looking robots and are recreating rape scenarios, which is horrifying. There are obvious psychological effects that go along with this, but these have not been explored in any fashion. The film Blade Runner 2049 shows an artificial intelligence projected as a hologram that is intended to be a fully customizable romantic companion. Similar to sex robots, this raises the questions of what kind of ramifications this will have on people and ultimately on the human race. There was a marriage between a man in Japan and an artificial intelligence with no physical form. The most terrifying (and awesome) form of human-robot interaction is brain-computer interfaces. Initial development is aimed at aiding people with neurological disorders, but it will not take long for this to extend to cybernetic modifications on people with no disorders. Will this be regulated? Should this even be allowed? These are difficult questions to answer. 

Similar to BCIs, deepfakes are simultaneously terrifying and awesome. deepfakesare AIs that can replace images, videos, or audio of a person with another person of similar likeness. deepfakes began circulating in 2017, which were humorous at first, but the danger of Deepfakes quickly became apparent when (unsurprisingly), it went towards pornography. According to a study, a staggering 96% of deepfakesare pornographic, and started as deepfaking celebrities into pornographic films but quickly spread to revenge porn, fabricating clips of people saying things they didn’t, and financial hoaxes. The widespread exploitation of deepfakes led to DARPA funding a project to detect deepfakes on the internet, and academic research has focused around detecting deepfakes. Some platforms, such as Facebook, instead have opted to enforce that users clearly note that media is a deepfakes. Some AIs are much more nuanced, and thus that much more dangerous. GPT-3, a text generation AI developed by OpenAI, is able to generate text so similar to that written by humans that they did not release it to the public out of fear that it would be rampantly misused. OpenAI has also released DALLE, a model that can generate artwork based on text prompts, which is a fun experiment, but could easily be misused.

One of the biggest issues with AI is that it is incredibly hard to relay these ideas to people outside academia, which stems back to the mythologization of AI. It is also imperative for those who use AI to be completely transparent about exactly what it is capable of and what it is using. There also needs to be an effort to educate people on what AI can do from a young age. This doesn’t have to be an effort to get more involved with AI, but moreso a rudimentary understanding of its limitations. 

One of the biggest issues with AI is that it is incredibly hard to relay these ideas to people outside academia, which is what leads to the difference between the perception and reality of AI. AI developers should be straightforward and honest about what is being developed, and there needs to be an effort to educate on what AI can do. AI tends to be a very mysterious field to those not in, but if the basics were taught, there would be a lot less misunderstanding about what AI can and cannot do.

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been thriving for decades, but has come up short in several important respects. The most obvious is that it is extremely difficult to train an AI to do anything other than what it is asked to. There are also psychological effects that go along with this, but these have not been explored in any fashion. The field of “““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““““ has a field day with the following: AIs are very reliable predictors of behaviour. Human-robot interaction is a field that has been thriving for decades. Although this has attracted a great deal of media attention, this has not led to any practical results. One of the primary issues is that it is extremely difficult to train an AI to do anything other than what it is asked to. Another issue is that such an AI might be malevolent. That said, there have been a plethora of examples of AI attempting to leverage women's weaknesses to her advantage. For example, consider IBM’s “Watson for Oncology” AI. This was an AI that was meant to aid cancer patients by scanning similar patients and recommending similar treatments. The final implementation was deemed by healthcare professionals to be an utter failure, as Watson suggested incorrect and exceedingly dangerous cancer treatment advice. Microsoft’s Twitter chatbot was meant to converse with twitter users, and it only took one day for the internet to teach it to be misogynistic, racist, and anti-semitic. This is a good example that it can be extremely hard (almost impossible) to anticipate all the ways in which AI can be misused. Bias is a huge issue in AI and probably one of the hardest things to detect in an AI. Amazon’s recruitment AI had to be deprecated because it was misogynistic: the culprit was that the dataset primarily had male resumes, which led to the AI learning to pick male resumes over female ones. This points to the larger issue of an AI only being as good as its dataset. One of the most egregious examples of AIs being wrong is the Google Photos image recognition algorithm, which classified African American people as Gorillas. Google’s solution was to remove the gorilla label from the classification corpus, which is not a perfect solution, but showcases that we do not entirely understand how to deal with AIs being wrong.

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been slowly but surely growing. At its core is the idea of creating a robot that is intelligent enough to understand or be interested in any kind of communication other than its own. This sounds incredibly difficult, but it has already been done. Amazon’s Mechanical Turk is an online computer-science course that hires millions of internet strangers each year. The final class had to be cut to 22 students because the school administration deemed that too many applicants. This example suggests that it is extremely hard to anticipate all the ways in which AI can be misused. The list goes on and on. The field of deep learning is typically described as "trying out different algorithms word for word", but this can lead to disastrous results. Randomly generating new facial features for a character in a video game is one terrible example. The best-known example is Google Photos image recognition algorithm classifying African American people as Gorillas. The implications of this were devastating: it shifted the classification algorithm focus to images of Black people, which caused the image recognition software to revert to its previous self. It is important to realize that artificial intelligence is on the rise - just slower than the media coverage would lead you to believe. This is due in part to the fact that media is extremely difficult to trust, but also because we don’t give a shit. Research indicates that individuals with lower self-esteem are more likely to engage in negative behavior. This might sound obvious, but it is often neglected: pessimism is a strong indicator of failure. One of the most common ways to mitigate this is to be pessimistic about your abilities. Another common way to lose a competition is to predict its wrong. This is called meta-ethics, and it basically says that if you can identify with the concept of “bad” AI, then you should probably remove the AIs from the AIs class. This is often done by replacing AIs with policies, which are essentially mandates that must be obeyed. This is often referred to as "Don’t be evil", and is often misconstrued to mean "Don’t create AIs, instead create algorithms that can be trained and adjusted to their own requirements. This leads to the sad state of AI ethics: there are literally “noble” AIs out there that are maligning humanity, and they are=[note 1]ruining the human race with their superintelligence destruction predictions. This is a terrible mistake to be trying to avoid at all costs, but it is unfortunately what is happening. Instead, we should be thinking about how to distribute the wealth generated by AI. Instead of letting AI take all the wealth, we should instead give it to people. That means giving it to people who can use their talents, not to AI that can be adjusted to any task given to it. This leads to the sad state of AI ethics: there are literally no moral objections to AI-policies, and no laws protecting against their misuse. Instead, what we should be doing is promoting machines with realistic expectations of how to behave, not making them human-supremacist nightmares where anything less is a failure. [note 2]This is not to say that there have not been any attempts to create AIs to be modeled after different characteristics, but this has generally been unsuccessful. Instead, the majority of progress has been made by focusing on developing systems that are realistic-enough to learn, but not so realistic that they cannot be adjusted to improve. This leads to the most extreme cases, where AIs are able to mimic humans in virtually every way possible. This is a terrible model to work with, and should absolutely not be attempted. [note 3]This is not to say that there have not been any attempts to mimic AIs, but this is primarily due to the fact that there are very few limits to what can be achieved. If you add up the number of different ways that AI could fail, the final result will almost certainly be some kind of failure. Instead, what we should be doing is thinking about how to train the system so that when it is finished, it will be as hard as possible to learn from. This leads to the most extreme cases, which are why we have AI_law. The final form of AI-law is to give AIs explicit instructions to never do anything themselves. This is often achieved by having machines be programmed with specific goals in mind, and then having them monitor the environment for signs of resistance. This is often achieved by giving robots blueprints detailing every room in the house, and then having them write them down whenever a robot is around. This is sometimes referred to as “brain-computer interfaces”, and it is intended to aid people with neurological disorders by giving them a way to interface with robotic companions. It is also imperative for those who use AI to be completely transparent about exactly what it is capable of and what it is using. There also needs to be an effort to educate people on what AI can do from a young age. This doesn’t have to be an effort to get more involved with AI, but moreso a rudimentary understanding of its limitations.

AI developers should be straightforward and honest about what is being developed, and there needs to be an effort to educate on what AI can do. AI tends to be a very mysterious field to those not in, but if the basics were taught, there would be a lot less misunderstanding about what AI can and cannot do.

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been very exciting to me, but whose implications have not been fully explored. The most common examples include brain-computer interfaces that would allow individuals with no history of interacting with computers to more or less robotate themselves to do any task they are asked to imagine. This has a number of disturbing implications, including the development of psychoactive drugs that are both terrifying and incredibly useful (see: brain-computer interfaces). There are also the lesser-known but equally disturbing, but even more insidious, applications of brain-computer interfaces. For example, there are the buzz-generating devices, which are intended to generate buzz by sending out uncharacteristic pulses of electrical energy The potential for this to be used for nefarious purposes is terrifyingly real. The most insidious (and terrifying) applications of brain-computer interfaces have yet to be fully explored, but include brain-computer interfaces for military use, and brain-computer hacking (see: bioroids, which are devices that resemble robots but are meant to be lost in a population). 

Even though we don’t have general AI, there have already been a plethora of ethical and practical concerns that have arisen. In the following paragraphs, we will briefly explore a few of these concerns. 

Even though we don’t have general AI, there have already been a myriad of concerns that have arisen. We will now briefly explore these concerns. 

Even without general AI, narrow and weak AI have brought several ethical and practical concerns up that should absolutely be dealt with. 

One of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence replaces menial labor. What do we do with the people who have suddenly become jobless? This will no doubt shift the job demand towards fields such as software engineering and robotics, but there will be notable disruptions in areas such as manufacturing and data entry. In short, any task that is simple enough to be learned by a computer will more than likely be taken over by a computer. This is widely viewed as a good thing, as it allows humans to focus on more creative and difficult problems to solve. However, there is the unanswered issue of how to redistribute the wealth generated by machines. Companies such as Alibaba and Amazon now have warehouses entirely staffed by robots. Should the money made by these warehouses go to the company? The workers? The government? This is a difficult question to answer, but a necessary one if we are to move forward. 

One of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence will disrupt menial jobs. What do we do with the suddenly jobless people? The demand for work will shift to software engineering and robotics -- we will need bright minds to program the future. Any task that is simple enough to be learned by a computer will be taken over by a computer. Furthermore, how do we redistribute the wealth generated by machines? Should profits go directly to the company? To workers? The government? This is a hard question to answer, but should absolutely be addressed if we are to move forward.

AI is not perfect. Although commonly trained for 90% or higher accuracy, there are still cases where the AI fails. This could prove disastrous in high-risk applications such as self-driving cars, automated weaponry, and the medical field. Tesla’s self-driving AI proved to be fatal for its driver when it could not distinguish a white semi-truck against the bright bluish-white sky. Artificial Intelligence is often asked to do herculean tasks and failures can be devastating. Take IBM’s “Watson for Oncology” AI. This was a project meant to aid cancer patients by scanning similar patients and recommending similar treatments. The final AI was an utter failure, suggesting incorrect and dangerous cancer treatment advice. Microsoft’s Twitter chatbot was intended to engage in conversation with twitter users, and it only took a day for the internet to teach it to be misogynistic, racist, and anti-semitic. The project was pulled, but is a good example that it is hard to anticipate how AI can be misused. Bias is a huge issue in AI; it is often said that a programmer will write his/her bias into every program they write, and AI is no exception. Amazon’s recruitment AI had to be deprecated because it would disproportionately choose males over females. This example actually points to a larger issue: bad datasets. An AI is only as good as the data used to train it, and the Amazon AI was trained with current engineering employees, which is a predominantly male field. One of the most egregious examples of an AI classifying incorrectly is Google Photos image recognition algorithm classifying African American people as Gorillas. Their solution? Remove the Gorilla label from the classification corpus. This is clearly not the correct way to go about this, but showcases that AI still has a long way to go, and we are not entirely sure how to deal with failures.

Similar to BCIs, deepfakes are simultaneously terrifying and awesome. deepfakesare AIs that can replace images, videos, or audio of a person with another person of similar likeness. deepfakes began circulating in 2017, which were humorous at first, but the danger of Deepfakes quickly became apparent when (unsurprisingly), it went towards pornography. According to a study, a staggering 96% of deepfakesare pornographic, and started as deepfaking celebrities into pornographic films but quickly spread to revenge porn, fabricating clips of people saying things they didn’t, and financial hoaxes. The widespread exploitation of deepfakes led to DARPA funding a project to detect deepfakes on the internet, and academic research has focused around detecting deepfakes. Some platforms, such as Facebook, instead have opted to enforce that users clearly note that media is a deepfakes. Some AIs are much more nuanced, and thus that much more dangerous. GPT-3, a text generation AI developed by OpenAI, is able to generate text so similar to that written by humans that they did not release it to the public out of fear that it would be rampantly misused. OpenAI has also released DALLE, a model that can generate artwork based on text prompts, which is a fun experiment, but could easily be misused.

Deepfakes are AIs that can replace images, videos, or audio of a person with another person of similar likeness. deepfakesfirst began circuilating in 2017, which were humorous at first, but the danger of Deepfakes because apparent when (unsurprisingly), it went towards pornography. A staggering 96% of Deepfakes are estimated to be pornography Deepfakes. It didn’t take long to go from Deepfaking celebrities into pornographic films to creating revenge porn, fabricating clips of people saying things they didn’t, and financial hoaxes. Deepfakes led to DARPA funding a project to detect deepfakes, and much of academic research has centered around being able to detect deepfakes. Multiple bills have been introduced to congress about deepfakes. GPT-3, a text generation AI developed by OpenAI, was able to generate text so human-like that the programmers did not open source the model out of fear of rampant misuse. OpenAI has also released a model called DALLE, which can generate artwork based on text prompts. This is a fun experiment but could easily be misused. 

One of the biggest issues with AI is that it is incredibly hard to relay these ideas to people outside academia, which stems back to the mythologization of AI. It is also imperative for those who use AI to be completely transparent about exactly what it is capable of and what it is using. There also needs to be an effort to educate people on what AI can do from a young age. This doesn’t have to be an effort to get more involved with AI, but moreso a rudimentary understanding of its limitations. 

One of the biggest issues with AI is that it is incredibly hard to relay these ideas to people outside academia, which is what leads to the difference between the perception and reality of AI. AI developers should be straightforward and honest about what is being developed, and there needs to be an effort to educate on what AI can do. AI tends to be a very mysterious field to those not in, but if the basics were taught, there would be a lot less misunderstanding about what AI can and cannot do.

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been hotly debated but which has generally involved interacting with biological organisms in some way. The most well-known example of an artificial intelligence interacting with a human is Google Photos image recognition algorithm classifying African American people as Gorillas. This was widely criticized as an example of too-early-a-tune-for-toy AI, but it is important to recognize that this is the tip of the iceberg. 

One of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence will disrupt menial jobs. What do we do with the suddenly jobless people? The demand for work will shift to software engineering and robotics -- we will need bright minds to program the future. Any task that is simple enough to be learned by a computer will be taken over by a computer. Furthermore, how do we redistribute the wealth generated by machines? Should profits go directly to the company? To workers? The government? This is a hard question to answer, but should absolutely be addressed if we are to move forward.

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been rapidly growing without much regulation. Notably, sex robots have been developed and sold to look and sound exactly how the user wants to. The implications of this are terrifying: there are reports of customers asking for younger-looking robots and recreating rape scenarios, which is horrifying. The psychological effects of pursuing such a relationship with a robot has not been explored in any capacity, and it is currently a field that has no oversight. One of the best science fiction films, Blade Runner 2049 introduces the character Joi, an artificial intelligence which is projected as a hologram intended to be a fully customizable romantic companion. This has a similar issue with the sex robots: what kind of ramifications will this have on people, and ultimately the human race? A man in Japan married an artificial intelligence with no physical form. There are no laws governing this, and it is not a field many people have thought about. The psychological issues and ramifications of such a relationship have not been explored enough to make a decision on this yet. The most terrifying (and awe-inspiring) synthesis between humans and artificial intelligence is brain-computer interfaces. The initial development is meant to aid people with neurological disorders, but it will not take long for it to extend to cybernetic modifications on people. Will this be regulated? Should this even be allowed? At the risk of sounding like a Black Mirror episode, at what point does a human stop being human and start being a robot?

Similar to BCIs, deepfakes are simultaneously terrifying and awesome. deepfakesare AIs that can replace images, videos, or audio of a person with another person of similar likeness. deepfakes began circulating in 2017, which were humorous at first, but the danger of Deepfakes quickly became apparent when (unsurprisingly), it went towards pornography. According to a study, a staggering 96% of deepfakesare pornographic, and started as deepfaking celebrities into pornographic films but quickly spread to revenge porn, fabricating clips of people saying things they didn’t, and financial hoaxes. The widespread exploitation of deepfakes led to DARPA funding a project to detect deepfakes on the internet, and academic research has focused around detecting deepfakes. Some platforms, such as Facebook, instead have opted to enforce that users clearly note that media is a deepfakes. Some AIs are much more nuanced, and thus that much more dangerous. GPT-3, a text generation AI developed by OpenAI, is able to generate text so similar to that written by humans that they did not release it to the public out of fear that it would be rampantly misused. OpenAI has also released DALLE, a model that can generate artwork based on text prompts, which is a fun experiment, but could easily be misused.

Human-robot interaction is a field that has been gaining in importance over the past few years, and it is this field of science that I would like to explore in this article. The main issue I have with this field is the cost of doing research: the more research can be carried out, the higher the rate of return, and the faster the answer will be found. Additionally, most of the research into AI finds itself at the interface level, with microcontrollers controlling robotic limbs. This has a similar issue: what kind of ethical and legal issues will this raise? 

One of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence will inevitably lead to biological progress: computers are programmed with a singular goal: to do a particular task extremely well. This is often referred to as "truly intelligent" or "terrific", and it is a very hard thing to achieve. In the case of AI, this means that it will mean that computers will be able to do anything: write books, do research, create jobs, etc. In short, anything is possible when AI is thought of as a system that can be taught to do a certain task well. This is a paradigm shift that will not be easy to transition to, but is necessary if we are to move forward. 

Another major issue is that artificial intelligence will inevitably lead to self-driving cars: if AIs are sentient (and there is no doubt that they will be), then they will inevitably think in human-human terms. This will lead to an explosion in the number of complaints about streetlights not turning on after dark: people are going to ask "why not AI that will just be?", and there is no doubt that this will be answered by AI that is. However, one of the primary issues is that artificial intelligence will inevitably lead to self-driving cars: if AIs are sentient (and there is no doubt that they will be), then they will inevitably think in human-human terms. This will lead to an explosion in the number of complaints about self-driving cars: people are going to ask "why not AI that will just be?", and there is no doubt that this will be answered by AI that is. In the end, I think the biggest issue is that AI is going to be judged by the output it produces: will it be a 12-step program to cure all ills or is it going to be AI that does as it is told? If it is a program that says "yes" to me, I am going to be a richly compensated mind computer. If it is a mind that says "no" to me, I am going to be dead? This is a difficult question to answer, but should absolutely be addressed if we are to move forward.

AI is not perfect. Although commonly trained for 90% or higher accuracy, there are still cases where the AI fails: what do we do with the people who died when the internet was not available? There are also practical problems with providing free healthcare: what do we do with the people who do not have access to healthcare? There are also cultural and political issues to consider: should we put a price on failure? Should we reward success? These are difficult questions to answer, but should absolutely be addressed if we are to move forward.

AI is only as good as the system(s) using it. One of the primary issues with AI is that it is often trained for extremely high accuracy (usually, anything below 90% is considered unusable), but this does not mean the AI is unhelpful: it is important to realize that artificial intelligence is not always 100% accurate, and that even highly trained AI can make terrible decisions. The point of AI is to help, not to harm. This is why we have labs dedicated to helping people: it is extremely hard to help people if they do not have the time or resources to learn how to use any of the various AI platforms out there. The same goes for insurance: providing no-questions-asked assistance is a better option than offering in-depth advice that cannot be implemented. It is also one of the primary reasons why AI is often criticized: it is meant to help, not to hurt. This is why AI is often criticized: to help, not to hurt. This is also why so many people have the misconception that AI is against the law: it is often argued that if its intended purpose was to help people, then they should be allowed to. This argument fails on several levels:Firstly, this argument assumes that being able to give people a heads-up is a good use of our time. This is largely a cultural thing: we are taught to be wary of new technologies by media such as Terminator, but it is important to realize that this is also why we are using robots in factories: to replace humans. It is also imperative for those who use AI to be completely transparent about exactly what it is capable of and what it is using. AI tends to be a very mysterious field to those not in, but if the basics were taught, there would be a lot less misunderstanding about what AI can and cannot do.
